<tr style="background:#f8eaba; border-top:2px solid transparent;">
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Chemical_infobox#References" title="Wikipedia:Chemical infobox">Infobox references</a></td>
</tr>
<tr style="background:#f8eaba;">
<td style="width:40%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-right:1px solid transparent;"></td>
<td style="width:60%; max-height:1px; border-top:2px solid transparent; border-left:1px solid transparent;"></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p><b>Nitric oxide</b> (<a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">nitrogen oxide</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> nitrogen monoxide) is a <a href="/wiki/Molecule" title="Molecule">molecular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chemical_compound" title="Chemical compound">chemical compound</a> with <a href="/wiki/Chemical_formula" title="Chemical formula">chemical formula</a> of <a href="/wiki/Unpaired_electron" title="Unpaired electron">·</a><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N</a><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a>. One of several <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> of nitrogen, it is a colorless gas under <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard conditions</a>. Nitric oxide is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free_radical" title="Free radical">free radical</a>—i.e., its bonding structure includes an <a href="/wiki/Unpaired_electron" title="Unpaired electron">unpaired electron</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> represented by the dot (·) on the nitrogen atom—and it is in the class of heteronuclear <a href="/wiki/Diatomic_molecule" title="Diatomic molecule">diatomic molecules</a> that are of historic theoretical interest (for the insights they gave in formulating early modern <a href="/wiki/Molecular_orbital_theory" title="Molecular orbital theory">theories of bonding</a>). It is a particularly important <a href="/wiki/Reaction_intermediate" title="Reaction intermediate">intermediate</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Chemical_industry" title="Chemical industry">chemical industry</a>. In addition, nitric oxide is formed in nitrogen-oxygen mixtures at high-temperature conditions, such as those found in combustion systems including <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion piston engines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gas_turbine" title="Gas turbine">gas turbines</a>, <a href="/wiki/Furnace" title="Furnace">industrial furnaces</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boilers</a>. It is also produced naturally by the extremely high air temperatures produced along the path of lightning in thunderstorms.</p>
<p>In mammals including humans, nitric oxide is an important cellular <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Signaling_molecule" title="Signaling molecule">signaling molecule</a> involved in many physiological and pathological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It is a powerful vasodilator with a short half-life of a few seconds in the blood. Long-known pharmaceuticals such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nitroglycerine" title="Nitroglycerine">nitroglycerine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amyl_nitrite" title="Amyl nitrite">amyl nitrite</a> were found to be precursors to nitric oxide more than a century after their first use in medicine. Low levels of nitric oxide production are important in protecting organs such as the liver from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ischemic_damage" title="Ischemic damage">ischemic damage</a>. Nitric oxide production is associated with nonalcoholic fatty liver disease (NAFLD) and is essential for hepatic lipid metabolism under starvation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a consequence of its importance in <a href="/wiki/Neuroscience" title="Neuroscience">neuroscience</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physiology" title="Physiology">physiology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Immunology" title="Immunology">immunology</a>, nitric oxide was proclaimed "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecule_of_the_Year" title="Molecule of the Year">Molecule of the Year</a>" in 1992.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid1361684_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid1361684-7">[7]</a></sup> Research into its function led to the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Nobel_laureates_in_Physiology_or_Medicine#1951.E2.80.932000" title="List of Nobel laureates in Physiology or Medicine">1998 Nobel Prize</a> for discovering the role of nitric oxide as a cardiovascular signalling molecule. Nitric oxide should not be confused with <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> (N<sub>2</sub>O), an <a href="/wiki/General_anaesthetic" title="General anaesthetic">anesthetic</a>, or with <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> (NO<sub>2</sub>), a brown <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toxic_gas" title="Toxic gas">toxic gas</a> and a major <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_pollutant" title="Air pollutant">air pollutant</a>, the latter being a product to which nitric oxide is rapidly oxidized in air.</p>
<p>It is classified as an <a href="/wiki/List_of_extremely_hazardous_substances" title="List of extremely hazardous substances">extremely hazardous substance</a> in the United States as defined in Section 302 of the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Emergency_Planning_and_Community_Right-to-Know_Act" title="Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act">Emergency Planning and Community Right-to-Know Act</a> (42 U.S.C. 11002), and is subject to strict reporting requirements by facilities which produce, store, or use it in significant quantities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gov-right-know_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-gov-right-know-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reactions">Reactions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Reactions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<ul>
<li>When exposed to <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, nitric oxide is converted into <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a>.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>2 ·NO + O<sub>2</sub> → 2 NO<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>This conversion has been speculated as occurring via the ONOONO intermediate. In water, nitric oxide reacts with oxygen and water to form HNO<sub>2</sub> or <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_acid" title="Nitrous acid">nitrous acid</a>. The reaction is thought to proceed via the following <a href="/wiki/Stoichiometry" title="Stoichiometry">stoichiometry</a>:
<dl>
<dd>4 ·NO + O<sub>2</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O → 4 HNO<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li>Nitric oxide will react with <a href="/wiki/Fluorine" title="Fluorine">fluorine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Bromine" title="Bromine">bromine</a> to form the XNO species, known as the nitrosyl halides, such as <a href="/wiki/Nitrosyl_chloride" title="Nitrosyl chloride">nitrosyl chloride</a>. Nitrosyl iodide can form but is an extremely short-lived species and tends to reform I<sub>2</sub>.</li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>2 ·NO + Cl<sub>2</sub> → 2 NOCl</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitroxyl" title="Nitroxyl">Nitroxyl</a> (HNO) is the reduced form of nitric oxide.</li>
<li>Nitric oxide dimer N<sub>2</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is formed when nitric oxide is cooled.</li>
<li>Nitric oxide reacts with <a href="/wiki/Acetone" title="Acetone">acetone</a> and an <a href="/wiki/Alkoxide" title="Alkoxide">alkoxide</a> to a <i>diazeniumdiolate</i> or <i>nitrosohydroxylamine</i> and <a href="/wiki/Methyl_acetate" title="Methyl acetate">methyl acetate</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<dl>
<dd><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TraubeReaction.svg" title="Traube reaction"><img alt="Traube reaction" data-file-height="209" data-file-width="565" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/TraubeReaction.svg/400px-TraubeReaction.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/TraubeReaction.svg/600px-TraubeReaction.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/TraubeReaction.svg/800px-TraubeReaction.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a></dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>This reaction was discovered around 1898, and remains of interest in nitric oxide <a href="/wiki/Prodrug" title="Prodrug">prodrug</a> research. Nitric oxide can also react directly with <a href="/wiki/Sodium_methoxide" title="Sodium methoxide">sodium methoxide</a>, forming <a href="/wiki/Sodium_formate" title="Sodium formate">sodium formate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Preparation">Preparation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Preparation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In commercial settings, nitric oxide is produced by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxidation" title="Oxidation">oxidation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> at 750–900 °C (normally at 850 °C) with <a href="/wiki/Platinum" title="Platinum">platinum</a> as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>4 NH<sub>3</sub> + 5 O<sub>2</sub> → 4 ·NO + 6 H<sub>2</sub>O</dd>
</dl>
<p>The uncatalyzed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a> reaction of <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> (O<sub>2</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> (N<sub>2</sub>), which is performed at high temperature (&gt;2000 °C) by lightning has not been developed into a practical commercial synthesis (see <a href="/wiki/Birkeland%E2%80%93Eyde_process" title="Birkeland–Eyde process">Birkeland–Eyde process</a>):</p>
<dl>
<dd>N<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> → 2 ·NO</dd>
</dl>
<p>In the laboratory, nitric oxide is conveniently generated by reduction of dilute <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> with <a href="/wiki/Copper" title="Copper">copper</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>8 HNO<sub>3</sub> + 3 Cu → 3 Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub> + 4 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 ·NO</dd>
</dl>
<p>or by the reduction of nitrous acid in the form of <a href="/wiki/Sodium_nitrite" title="Sodium nitrite">sodium nitrite</a> or <a href="/wiki/Potassium_nitrite" title="Potassium nitrite">potassium nitrite</a>:</p>
<dl>
<dd>2 NaNO<sub>2</sub> + 2 NaI + 2 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → I<sub>2</sub> + 4 NaHSO<sub>4</sub> + 2 ·NO</dd>
<dd>2 NaNO<sub>2</sub> + 2 FeSO<sub>4</sub> + 3 H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub> → Fe<sub>2</sub>(SO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> + 2 NaHSO<sub>4</sub> + 2 H<sub>2</sub>O + 2 ·NO</dd>
<dd>3 KNO<sub>2(l)</sub> + KNO<sub>3(l)</sub> + Cr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3(s)</sub> → 2 K<sub>2</sub>CrO<sub>4(s)</sub> + 4 NO<sub>(g)</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>The iron(II) sulfate route is simple and has been used in undergraduate laboratory experiments. So-called <a href="/wiki/NONOate" title="NONOate">NONOate</a> compounds are also used for nitric oxide generation.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coordination_chemistry">Coordination chemistry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Coordination chemistry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_nitrosyl" title="Metal nitrosyl">Metal nitrosyl</a></div>
<p>Nitric oxide reacts with all <a href="/wiki/Transition_metal" title="Transition metal">transition metals</a> to give complexes called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_nitrosyl" title="Metal nitrosyl">metal nitrosyls</a>. The most common bonding mode of nitric oxide is the terminal linear type (M−NO). The angle of the M−N−O group varies from 160° to 180° but is still termed "linear". In this case, the nitric oxide group is considered a 3-electron donor under the covalent (neutral) method of electron counting, or a 2-electron donor under the ionic method.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> One can view such complexes as derived from NO<sup>+</sup>, which is isoelectronic with <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO).</p>
<p>In the case of a bent M−N−O conformation, the ·NO group can be considered a one-electron donor using neutral counting, or a 2-electron donor using ionic counting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nitric oxide can serve as a one-electron pseudohalide. In such complexes, the M−N−O group is characterized by an angle between 120° and 140°.</p>
<p>The ·NO group can also bridge between metal centers through the nitrogen atom in a variety of geometries.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Concentration_measurement">Concentration measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Concentration measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_(NO)_(white)_in_the_cytoplasm_(green)_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="252" data-file-width="401" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg/250px-The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg/375px-The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/06/The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_%28NO%29_%28white%29_in_the_cytoplasm_%28green%29_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_production_and_diffusion_of_nitric_oxide_(NO)_(white)_in_the_cytoplasm_(green)_of_clusters_of_conifer_cells_one_hour_after_mechanical_agitation.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Nitric oxide (white) in <a href="/wiki/Pinophyta" title="Pinophyta">conifer</a> cells, visualized using DAF-2 DA (diaminofluorescein diacetate)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Nitric oxide concentration can be determined using a simple <a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescent reaction</a> involving <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> A sample containing nitric oxide is mixed with a large quantity of ozone. The nitric oxide reacts with the ozone to produce <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a>, accompanied with emission of <a href="/wiki/Light" title="Light">light</a> (<a href="/wiki/Chemiluminescence" title="Chemiluminescence">chemiluminescence</a>):</p>
<dl>
<dd>·NO + O<sub>3</sub> → NO<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub> + <i>hν</i></dd>
</dl>
<p>which can be measured with a <a href="/wiki/Photodetector" title="Photodetector">photodetector</a>. The amount of light produced is proportional to the amount of nitric oxide in the sample.</p>
<p>Other methods of testing include <a href="/wiki/Electrochemistry" title="Electrochemistry">electroanalysis</a> (amperometric approach), where ·NO reacts with an electrode to induce a current or voltage change. The detection of NO radicals in biological tissues is particularly difficult due to the short lifetime and concentration of these radicals in tissues. One of the few practical methods is <a href="/wiki/Spin_trapping" title="Spin trapping">spin trapping</a> of nitric oxide with iron-<a href="/wiki/Dithiocarbamate" title="Dithiocarbamate">dithiocarbamate</a> complexes and subsequent detection of the mono-nitrosyl-iron complex with <a href="/wiki/Electron_paramagnetic_resonance" title="Electron paramagnetic resonance">electron paramagnetic resonance</a> (EPR).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>A group of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fluorescent_dye" title="Fluorescent dye">fluorescent dye</a> indicators that are also available in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acetyl" title="Acetyl">acetylated</a> form for intracellular measurements exist. The most common compound is <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=4,5-diaminofluorescein&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="4,5-diaminofluorescein (page does not exist)">4,5-diaminofluorescein</a> (DAF-2).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-undefined_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-undefined-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>From a thermodynamic perspective, ·NO is unstable with respect to O<sub>2</sub> and N<sub>2</sub>, although this conversion is very slow at ambient temperatures in the absence of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catalyst" title="Catalyst">catalyst</a>. Because the heat of formation of ·NO is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endothermic" title="Endothermic">endothermic</a>, its synthesis from molecular nitrogen and oxygen requires elevated temperatures above 1000 °C.</p>
<p>A major natural source is <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>. The use of <a href="/wiki/Internal_combustion_engine" title="Internal combustion engine">internal combustion engines</a> has drastically increased the presence of nitric oxide in the environment. One purpose of <a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">catalytic converters</a> in cars is to minimize ·NO emission by catalytic reversion to O<sub>2</sub> and N<sub>2</sub>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_effects">Environmental effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Environmental effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/NOx" title="NOx">NOx</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acid_deposition">Acid deposition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Acid deposition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitric oxide reacts with the hydroperoxy radical (HO<sub>2</sub><sup>•</sup>) to form nitrogen dioxide (NO<sub>2</sub>), which then can react with a hydroxyl radical (<sup><a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">•</a></sup><a href="/wiki/Hydroxyl_radical" title="Hydroxyl radical">OH</a>) to produce <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> (HNO<sub>3</sub>):</p>
<dl>
<dd>·NO + HO<sub>2</sub><sup>•</sup>→ <sup>•</sup>NO<sub>2</sub> + <sup>•</sup>OH</dd>
<dd>·NO<sub>2</sub> + <sup>•</sup>OH → HNO<sub>3</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>Nitric acid, along with <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, contribute <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a> deposition.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ozone_depletion">Ozone depletion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ozone depletion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Furthermore, ·NO participates in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozone_layer_depletion" title="Ozone layer depletion">ozone layer depletion</a>. In this process, nitric oxide reacts with stratospheric <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> to form O<sub>2</sub> and nitrogen dioxide:</p>
<dl>
<dd>·NO + O<sub>3</sub> → NO<sub>2</sub> + O<sub>2</sub></dd>
</dl>
<p>As seen in the Concentration Measurement section, this reaction is also utilized to measure concentrations of ·NO in control volumes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Precursor_to_NO2">Precursor to NO<sub>2</sub></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Precursor to NO2">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As seen in the Acid deposition section, nitric oxide can transform into nitrogen dioxide (this can happen with the hydroperoxy radical, HO<sub>2</sub><sup>•</sup>, or diatomic oxygen, O<sub>2</sub>). Symptoms of short-term nitrogen dioxide exposure include nausea, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyspnea" title="Dyspnea">dyspnea</a> and headache. Long-term effects could include impaired immune and <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_system" title="Respiratory system">respiratory</a> function.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Technical_applications">Technical applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Technical applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although ·NO has relatively few direct uses, it is produced on a massive scale as an intermediate in the <a href="/wiki/Ostwald_process" title="Ostwald process">Ostwald process</a> for the synthesis of <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a>. In 2005, the US alone produced 6 million metric tons of nitric acid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> It finds use in the <a href="/wiki/Semiconductor" title="Semiconductor">semiconductor</a> industry for various processes. In one of its applications, it is used along with <a href="/wiki/Nitrous_oxide" title="Nitrous oxide">nitrous oxide</a> to form <a href="/wiki/Silicon_oxynitride" title="Silicon oxynitride">oxynitride</a> gates in <a href="/wiki/CMOS" title="CMOS">CMOS</a> devices.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Miscellaneous_applications">Miscellaneous applications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Miscellaneous applications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitric oxide can be used for detecting surface radicals on polymers. Quenching of surface <a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)" title="Radical (chemistry)">radicals</a> with nitric oxide results in incorporation of nitrogen, which can be quantified by means of <a href="/wiki/X-ray_photoelectron_spectroscopy" title="X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy">X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biological_functions">Biological functions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Biological functions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Biological_functions_of_nitric_oxide" title="Biological functions of nitric oxide">Biological functions of nitric oxide</a></div>
<p>NO is one of the few <a href="/wiki/Gaseous_signaling_molecules" title="Gaseous signaling molecules">gaseous signaling molecules</a> known and is additionally exceptional due to the fact that it is a radical gas. It is a key <a href="/wiki/Vertebrate" title="Vertebrate">vertebrate</a> <a href="/wiki/Signal_transduction" title="Signal transduction">biological messenger</a>, playing a role in a variety of biological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> It is a known bioproduct in almost all types of organisms, ranging from bacteria to plants, fungi, and animal cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nitric oxide, known as an <a href="/wiki/Endothelium-derived_relaxing_factor" title="Endothelium-derived relaxing factor">endothelium-derived relaxing factor</a> (EDRF), is biosynthesized endogenously from <a href="/wiki/Arginine" title="Arginine"><small>L</small>-arginine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NADPH" title="NADPH">NADPH</a> by various <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide_synthase" title="Nitric oxide synthase">nitric oxide synthase</a> (NOS) <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzymes</a>. Reduction of inorganic nitrate may also serve to make nitric oxide. The <a href="/wiki/Endothelium" title="Endothelium">endothelium</a> (inner lining) of <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessels</a> uses nitric oxide to signal the surrounding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">smooth muscle</a> to relax, thus resulting in <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a> and increasing blood flow. Nitric oxide is highly reactive (having a lifetime of a few seconds), yet diffuses freely across membranes. These attributes make nitric oxide ideal for a transient <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paracrine" title="Paracrine">paracrine</a> (between adjacent cells) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Autocrine" title="Autocrine">autocrine</a> (within a single cell) signaling molecule.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stryer_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-stryer-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>Independent of <a href="/wiki/Nitric_oxide_synthase" title="Nitric oxide synthase">nitric oxide synthase</a>, an alternative pathway, coined the nitrate-nitrite-nitric oxide pathway, elevates nitric oxide through the sequential reduction of dietary nitrate derived from plant-based foods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Nitrate-rich vegetables, in particular leafy greens, such as <a href="/wiki/Spinach" title="Spinach">spinach</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arugula" title="Arugula">arugula</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beetroot" title="Beetroot">beetroot</a>, have been shown to increase cardioprotective levels of nitric oxide with a corresponding reduction in blood pressure in pre-<a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertensive</a> persons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> For the body to generate nitric oxide through the nitrate-nitrite-nitric oxide pathway, the reduction of nitrate to nitrite (by <a href="/wiki/Nitrate_reductase" title="Nitrate reductase">nitrate reductase</a>, a bacterial enzyme) occurs in the mouth, by commensal bacteria, an obligatory and necessary step.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Monitoring nitric oxide status by <a href="/wiki/Saliva_testing" title="Saliva testing">saliva testing</a> detects the bioconversion of plant-derived nitrate into nitric oxide. A rise in salivary levels is indicative of diets rich in leafy vegetables which are often abundant in anti-hypertensive diets such as the <a href="/wiki/DASH_diet" title="DASH diet">DASH diet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<p>The production of nitric oxide is elevated in populations living at high altitudes, which helps these people avoid <a href="/wiki/Hypoxia_(medical)" title="Hypoxia (medical)">hypoxia</a> by aiding in pulmonary vasculature <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>. Effects include vasodilatation, <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">neurotransmission</a> (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gasotransmitters" title="Gasotransmitters">gasotransmitters</a>), modulation of the <a href="/wiki/Human_hair_growth" title="Human hair growth">hair cycle</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> production of reactive nitrogen intermediates and <a href="/wiki/Erection" title="Erection">penile erections</a> (through its ability to <a href="/wiki/Vascular_resistance" title="Vascular resistance">vasodilate</a>). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glyceryl_trinitrate_(pharmacology)" title="Glyceryl trinitrate (pharmacology)">Nitroglycerin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amyl_nitrite" title="Amyl nitrite">amyl nitrite</a> serve as vasodilators because they are converted to nitric oxide in the body. The vasodilating antihypertensive drug <a href="/wiki/Minoxidil" title="Minoxidil">minoxidil</a> contains an ·NO moiety and may act as an NO agonist. Likewise, <a href="/wiki/Sildenafil" title="Sildenafil">Sildenafil citrate</a>, popularly known by the trade name <i>Viagra</i>, stimulates erections primarily by enhancing signaling through the nitric oxide pathway in the penis.</p>
<p>Nitric oxide (NO) contributes to vessel homeostasis by inhibiting vascular smooth muscle contraction and growth, platelet aggregation, and leukocyte adhesion to the endothelium. Humans with <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diabetes" title="Diabetes">diabetes</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Hypertension" title="Hypertension">hypertension</a> often show impaired NO pathways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> A high salt intake was demonstrated to attenuate NO production in patients with essential hypertension, although bioavailability remains unregulated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nitric oxide is also generated by phagocytes (<a href="/wiki/Monocyte" title="Monocyte">monocytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Macrophage" title="Macrophage">macrophages</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Neutrophil" title="Neutrophil">neutrophils</a>) as part of the human <a href="/wiki/Immune_response" title="Immune response">immune response</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Phagocytes are armed with inducible nitric oxide synthase (iNOS), which is activated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interferon-gamma" title="Interferon-gamma">interferon-gamma</a> (IFN-γ) as a single signal or by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tumor_necrosis_factor" title="Tumor necrosis factor">tumor necrosis factor</a> (TNF) along with a second signal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> On the other hand, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Transforming_growth_factor-beta" title="Transforming growth factor-beta">transforming growth factor-beta</a> (TGF-β) provides a strong inhibitory signal to iNOS, whereas <a href="/wiki/Interleukin" title="Interleukin">interleukin</a>-4 (IL-4) and IL-10 provide weak inhibitory signals. In this way, the immune system may regulate the armamentarium of phagocytes that play a role in inflammation and immune responses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Nitric oxide is secreted as free radicals in an immune response and is toxic to bacteria and intracellular parasites, including <i><a href="/wiki/Leishmania" title="Leishmania">Leishmania</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> the mechanism for this includes DNA damage<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> and degradation of iron sulfur centers into iron ions and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metal_nitrosyl" title="Metal nitrosyl">iron-nitrosyl</a> compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>The inducible pathway (iNOS) of nitrogen oxide synthesis in phagocytes can generate large amounts of <a href="/wiki/Unpaired_electron" title="Unpaired electron">·</a><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N</a><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a> that trigger <a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a> in and thereby the death of cells. In vitro studies indicate that phagocyte-dependent generation of <a href="/wiki/Unpaired_electron" title="Unpaired electron">·</a><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N</a><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a> at concentrations greater than 400-500 NM triggers apoptosis in nearby cells and that this effect may act in a manner similar to <a href="/wiki/Specialized_pro-resolving_mediators" title="Specialized pro-resolving mediators">Specialized pro-resolving mediators</a> to dampen and reverse inflammatory responses by neutralizing and then speeding the clearance of pro-inflammatory cells from inflamed tissues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26095908_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26095908-43">[43]</a></sup> However, the role of <a href="/wiki/Unpaired_electron" title="Unpaired electron">·</a><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">N</a><a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">O</a> in inflammation is complex with model studies involving viral infection suggesting that this gaseous mediator can also promote inflammation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid26208702_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid26208702-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response, many bacterial pathogens have evolved mechanisms for nitric oxide resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Because nitric oxide might serve as an <i>inflammometer</i> (meter of inflammation) in conditions like <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a>, there has been increasing interest in the use of <a href="/wiki/Exhaled_nitric_oxide" title="Exhaled nitric oxide">exhaled nitric oxide</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Breath_test" title="Breath test">breath test</a> in diseases with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airway" title="Airway">airway</a> inflammation. Reduced levels of exhaled NO have been associated with exposure to air pollution in cyclists and smokers, but, in general, increased levels of exhaled NO are associated with exposure to air pollution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jacobs_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobs-46">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nitric oxide can contribute to <a href="/wiki/Reperfusion_injury" title="Reperfusion injury">reperfusion injury</a> when an excessive amount produced during reperfusion (following a period of <a href="/wiki/Ischemia" title="Ischemia">ischemia</a>) reacts with <a href="/wiki/Superoxide" title="Superoxide">superoxide</a> to produce the damaging oxidant <a href="/wiki/Peroxynitrite" title="Peroxynitrite">peroxynitrite</a>. In contrast, inhaled nitric oxide has been shown to help survival and recovery from <a href="/wiki/Paraquat" title="Paraquat">paraquat</a> poisoning, which produces lung tissue-damaging superoxide and hinders NOS metabolism.</p>
<p>In plants, nitric oxide can be produced by any of four routes: (i) L-arginine-dependent nitric oxide synthase,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> (although the existence of animal NOS homologs in plants is debated),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> (ii) plasma membrane-bound <a href="/wiki/Nitrate_reductase" title="Nitrate reductase">nitrate reductase</a>, (iii) mitochondrial electron transport chain, or (iv) non-enzymatic reactions. It is a signaling molecule, acts mainly against <a href="/wiki/Oxidative_stress" title="Oxidative stress">oxidative stress</a> and also plays a role in plant pathogen interactions. Treating cut flowers and other plants with nitric oxide has been shown to lengthen the time before wilting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two important biological reaction mechanisms of nitric oxide are S-<a href="/wiki/Nitrosation" title="Nitrosation">nitrosation</a> of thiols, and nitrosylation of transition metal ions. S-nitrosation involves the (reversible) conversion of <a href="/wiki/Thiol" title="Thiol">thiol</a> groups, including <a href="/wiki/Cysteine" title="Cysteine">cysteine</a> residues in proteins, to form S-nitrosothiols (RSNOs). S-<a href="/wiki/Nitrosation" title="Nitrosation">Nitrosation</a> is a mechanism for dynamic, post-translational regulation of most or all major classes of protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> The second mechanism, nitrosylation, involves the binding of ·NO to a transition metal ion like iron or copper. In this function, ·NO is referred to as a nitrosyl ligand. Typical cases involve the nitrosylation of heme proteins like cytochromes, thereby disabling the normal enzymatic activity of the enzyme. Nitrosylated ferrous iron is particularly stable, as the binding of the nitrosyl ligand to ferrous iron (Fe(II)) is very strong. Hemoglobin is a prominent example of a heme protein that may be modified by ·NO by both pathways: ·NO may attach directly to the heme in the nitrosylation reaction, and independently form S-nitrosothiols by S-nitrosation of the thiol moieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are several mechanisms by which ·NO has been demonstrated to affect the biology of living cells. These include oxidation of iron-containing proteins such as <a href="/wiki/Ribonucleotide_reductase" title="Ribonucleotide reductase">ribonucleotide reductase</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aconitase" title="Aconitase">aconitase</a>, activation of the soluble <a href="/wiki/Guanylate_cyclase" title="Guanylate cyclase">guanylate cyclase</a>, ADP ribosylation of proteins, protein sulfhydryl group <a href="/wiki/Nitrosylation" title="Nitrosylation">nitrosylation</a>, and iron regulatory factor activation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> ·NO has been demonstrated to activate <a href="/wiki/NF-%CE%BAB" title="NF-κB">NF-κB</a> in peripheral blood mononuclear cells, an important transcription factor in iNOS gene expression in response to inflammation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<p>It was found that ·NO acts through the stimulation of the soluble guanylate cyclase, which is a heterodimeric enzyme with subsequent formation of cyclic-GMP. Cyclic-GMP activates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protein_kinase_G" title="Protein kinase G">protein kinase G</a>, which causes reuptake of Ca<sup>2+</sup> and the opening of calcium-activated potassium channels. The fall in concentration of Ca<sup>2+</sup> ensures that the myosin light-chain kinase (MLCK) can no longer phosphorylate the myosin molecule, thereby stopping the crossbridge cycle and leading to relaxation of the smooth muscle cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical_use">Medical use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Medical use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neonatal_use">Neonatal use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Neonatal use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitric oxide/oxygen blends are used in critical care to promote capillary and pulmonary dilation to treat primary <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_hypertension" title="Pulmonary hypertension">pulmonary hypertension</a> in neonatal patients<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> and post-meconium aspiration related to birth defects. These are often a last-resort gas mixture before the use of <a href="/wiki/Extracorporeal_membrane_oxygenation" title="Extracorporeal membrane oxygenation">extracorporeal membrane oxygenation</a> (ECMO). Nitric oxide therapy has the potential to significantly increase the quality of life and, in some cases, save the lives of infants at risk for pulmonary vascular disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pediatric_and_adult_use">Pediatric and adult use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pediatric and adult use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The primary use is in the form of <a href="/wiki/Nitroglycerin_(drug)" title="Nitroglycerin (drug)">nitroglycerin</a>, either pill or liquid spray forms, which, as a prodrug, is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Denitration" title="Denitration">denitrated</a> and releases the active metabolite nitric oxide (NO).</p>
<p>As with all supplements of nitric oxide, the response is short-lived because, as a normally produced internal physiologic control mechanism, increased concentrations lead to increased rates of clearance, which is the reason that the effectiveness of sustained use of nitroglycerin for vasodilation fades to none after hours to days.</p>
<p>In the United States, ongoing direct use of nitric oxide use is only approved for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neonates" title="Neonates">neonates</a>. In the adult ICU setting, inhaled ·NO can improve hypoxemia in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_lung_injury" title="Acute lung injury">acute lung injury</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a>, and severe <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_hypertension" title="Pulmonary hypertension">pulmonary hypertension</a>, although the effects are short-lived and there are no studies demonstrating improved clinical outcomes. It is used on an individualized basis in ICUs as an adjunct to other definitive therapies for reversible causes of hypoxemic respiratory distress.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dosage_and_strength">Dosage and strength</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Dosage and strength">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Currently in the United States, nitric oxide is a gas available in concentrations of only 100 ppm and 800 ppm. Overdosage with inhaled nitric oxide will be seen by elevations in <a href="/wiki/Methemoglobin" title="Methemoglobin">methemoglobin</a> and pulmonary toxicities associated with inspired ·NO. Elevated NO may cause <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acute_lung_injury" title="Acute lung injury">acute lung injury</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contraindications">Contraindications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Contraindications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Inhaled nitric oxide is contraindicated in the treatment of neonates known to be dependent on right-to-left shunting of blood. This is as the nitric oxide decreases the pulmonary circulation's resistance by dilating pulmonary blood vessels. The increased pulmonary return increases pressure in the left atrium, causing closure of the foramen ovale and reducing the blood flow through the ductus arteriosus. Closing these shunts can kill neonates with heart malformations that rely on the right-to-left shunting of blood.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pulmonary_embolism">Pulmonary embolism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Pulmonary embolism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitric oxide is also administered as <a href="/wiki/Salvage_therapy" title="Salvage therapy">salvage therapy</a> in patients with acute <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_ventricular_failure" title="Right ventricular failure">right ventricular failure</a> secondary to <a href="/wiki/Pulmonary_embolism" title="Pulmonary embolism">pulmonary embolism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid22005573_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid22005573-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacology">Pharmacology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Pharmacology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitric oxide is considered an <a href="/wiki/Antianginal" title="Antianginal">anti</a><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angina_pectoris" title="Angina pectoris">anginal</a> drug: It causes <a href="/wiki/Vasodilation" title="Vasodilation">vasodilation</a>, which can help with ischemic pain, known as angina, by decreasing the cardiac workload. By dilating (expanding) the arteries, nitric oxide drugs lower arterial pressure and left ventricular filling pressure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Abrams_1996_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Abrams_1996-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<p>This vasodilation does not decrease the volume of blood the heart pumps, but rather it decreases the force the heart muscle must exert to pump the same volume of blood. Nitroglycerin pills, taken sublingually (under the tongue), are used to prevent or treat acute chest pain. The nitroglycerin reacts with a <a href="/wiki/Thiol" title="Thiol">sulfhydryl</a> group (–SH) to produce nitric oxide, which eases the pain by causing vasodilation. There is a potential role for the use of nitric oxide in alleviating bladder contractile dysfunctions,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Moro_et_al._2013_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Moro_et_al._2013-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andersson_et_al._2008_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andersson_et_al._2008-64">[64]</a></sup> and recent evidence suggests that nitrates may be beneficial for treatment of angina due to reduced myocardial oxygen consumption both by decreasing preload and afterload and by some direct vasodilation of coronary vessels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jonathan_Abrams_1996_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-Jonathan_Abrams_1996-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Associated_problems">Associated problems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Associated problems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>There are some associated complaints with utilization of nitric oxide in neonatal patients. Some of them include dose errors associated with the delivery system, headaches associated with environmental exposure of nitric oxide in hospital staff, <a href="/wiki/Hypotension" title="Hypotension">hypotension</a> associated with acute withdrawal of the drug, hypoxemia associated with acute withdrawal of the drug, and pulmonary edema in patients with CREST syndrome.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mechanism_of_action_2">Mechanism of action</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Mechanism of action">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nitric oxide is a compound produced by many cells of the body. It relaxes vascular smooth muscle by binding to the heme moiety of cytosolic guanylate cyclase, activating guanylate cyclase and increasing intracellular levels of cyclic-guanosine 3’,5’-monophosphate, which then leads to vasodilation. When inhaled, nitric oxide dilates the pulmonary vasculature and, because of efficient scavenging by hemoglobin, has minimal effect on the vasculature of the entire body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16870914_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16870914-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Inhaled nitric oxide appears to increase the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Partial_pressure_of_arterial_oxygen" title="Partial pressure of arterial oxygen">partial pressure of arterial oxygen</a> (P<sub>a</sub>O<sub>2</sub>) by dilating pulmonary vessels in better-ventilated areas of the lung, moving pulmonary blood flow away from lung segments with low ventilation/perfusion (V/Q) ratios toward segments with normal or better ratios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16870913_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid16870913-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pharmacokinetics">Pharmacokinetics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Pharmacokinetics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Nitric oxide is absorbed systemically after inhalation. Most of it moves across the pulmonary capillary bed where it combines with hemoglobin that is 60% to 100% oxygen-saturated.</p>
<p>Nitrate has been identified as the predominant nitric oxide metabolite excreted in the urine, accounting for &gt;70% of the nitric oxide dose inhaled. <a href="/wiki/Nitrate" title="Nitrate">Nitrate</a> is cleared from the plasma by the kidney at rates approaching the rate of glomerular filtration.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a result of its broad antibacterial properties as well as high penetrability in cells, as of 2007 inhaled nitric oxide therapy was being studied as a potential therapeutic intervention in acute and chronic lung infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Occupational_safety_and_health">Occupational safety and health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nitric_oxide&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Occupational safety and health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>People can be exposed to nitric oxide in the workplace by breathing it in. The <a href="/wiki/Occupational_Safety_and_Health_Administration" title="Occupational Safety and Health Administration">Occupational Safety and Health Administration</a> (OSHA) has set the legal limit (<a href="/wiki/Permissible_exposure_limit" title="Permissible exposure limit">permissible exposure limit</a>) for nitric oxide exposure in the workplace as 25 ppm (30 mg/m<sup>3</sup>) over an 8-hour workday. The <a href="/wiki/National_Institute_for_Occupational_Safety_and_Health" title="National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health">National Institute for Occupational Safety and Health</a> (NIOSH) has set a <a href="/wiki/Recommended_exposure_limit" title="Recommended exposure limit">recommended exposure limit</a> (REL) of 25 ppm (30 mg/m<sup>3</sup>) over an 8-hour workday. At levels of 100 ppm, nitric oxide is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IDLH" title="IDLH">immediately dangerous to life and health</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p><br/></p>

</ul>
<ul>
<li><i>Indirect/downstream NO modulators:</i> <a href="/wiki/ACE_inhibitor" title="ACE inhibitor">ACE inhibitors</a>/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angiotensin_II_receptor_antagonist" title="Angiotensin II receptor antagonist">AT-II receptor antagonists</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Captopril" title="Captopril">captopril</a>, <a href="/wiki/Losartan" title="Losartan">losartan</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Endothelin_receptor_antagonist" title="Endothelin receptor antagonist">ET<sub>B</sub> receptor antagonists</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Bosentan" title="Bosentan">bosentan</a>)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/L-Type_calcium_channel" title="L-Type calcium channel">L-Type calcium channel</a> <a href="/wiki/Calcium_channel_blocker" title="Calcium channel blocker">blockers</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Dihydropyridine" title="Dihydropyridine">dihydropyridines</a>: <a href="/wiki/Nifedipine" title="Nifedipine">nifedipine</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nebivolol" title="Nebivolol">Nebivolol</a> (<a href="/wiki/Beta_blocker" title="Beta blocker">beta blocker</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/PDE5_inhibitor" title="PDE5 inhibitor">PDE5 inhibitors</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Sildenafil" title="Sildenafil">sildenafil</a>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Statin" title="Statin">Statins</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Simvastatin" title="Simvastatin">simvastatin</a>)</li>
</ul>
